Tau as a compiled language for agentic workflows

Status: Vision document — not a SemVer-binding contract. This document describes the long-term design intent of the tau project. Individual elements may be realized incrementally over many sub- projects; some may be revised as the project matures.

Date: 2026-05-02 (initial draft alongside Tier 3 priority 12 — sandboxing).

Audience: project contributors, ADR authors, and users who want to understand where tau is heading beyond v0.1.

The proposition

Tau is a runtime + toolchain for agentic workflows that aspires to the discipline of a compiled language. A user writes a "tau program" once — a project tau.toml declaring agents, their package dependencies, capabilities, and (eventually) workflows — and the toolchain compiles that program for a specific target. If compilation succeeds, execution on that target is guaranteed to be sandbox-correct, capability-correct, and dependency-resolved.

The reference is Rust:

"If your code compiles, it runs. If it ran on x86_64-linux-gnu, it'll run on any x86_64-linux-gnu."

The tau analogue:

"If tau check --target <triple> succeeds, tau run --sandbox <triple> will not fail with a sandbox-, capability-, or dependency-resolution error on any host whose available adapter matches the target."

The remaining failure surface is genuine runtime behavior — LLM backend errors, tool dispatch errors, agent decisions — which is deliberately recoverable through the RunOutcome::Failed and RunEvent::FatalError paths already in the runtime. What gets eliminated by compilation is the class of "this configuration was never going to work" errors that surface mid-run today.

What is a "tau program"?

flowchart LR
    subgraph S["source"]
        T["project tau.toml<br/>agent decls<br/>capability overrides"]
        P["plugin packages<br/>each with tau.toml<br/>+ CAPABILITIES"]
        L["lockfile<br/>resolved versions<br/>+ tree hashes"]
    end
    S --> C{{"<code>tau check</code> +<br/><code>tau build --target &lt;triple&gt;</code>"}}
    C --> B["bundle.tau<br/>(content-hashed)<br/>pinned versions +<br/>effective capabilities +<br/>target triple"]
    B --> R["<code>tau run --bundle</code><br/>on a host matching<br/>the pinned target"]
    T -.-> P
    P -.-> L

The tau-language source for a project is the union of:

  1. Project tau.toml — agent declarations, [[requires.tools]] dependencies, capability overrides, sandbox tier preferences.
  2. All required plugin packages — including each plugin's own tau.toml manifest and embedded CAPABILITIES constant.
  3. Lockfile — the resolved version of every dependency (<scope>/lock.toml), produced by tau install / tau resolve.

The tau-language target is a sandbox target triple that specifies the execution environment's enforcement model and capability surface. Examples:

  • linux-native-strict — landlock + seccomp + namespaces; full per-host network filtering and per-command exec gating.
  • linux-native-light — landlock only; binary network gating; binary exec gating.
  • container-podman — Podman container with bind-mount filesystem capabilities and network namespace egress filtering.
  • remote-vercel — managed cloud sandbox (e.g., Vercel Sandbox).
  • wasi-p2 — WebAssembly System Interface preview 2 (future).

The tau-language artifact of compilation is a "tau application bundle" — a content-hashed deployment unit pinning:

  • All resolved package versions + their tree hashes (priority 7 primitive: ADR-0012's whole-tree SHA-256).
  • The compiled capability-effective set per agent (priority 4 primitive: compute_effective).
  • The required capability shapes per plugin (this priority's contribution).
  • The target sandbox triple.
  • A bundle-level SHA pinning the whole compilation.

The tau-language execution is tau run --bundle <bundle.tau> (or its streaming/REPL counterparts) on a host whose available sandbox target matches the bundle's pinned target.

What gets caught at "compile time"?

The validation hierarchy designed in priority 12's ADR-0014 already maps onto a compile-time / link-time / runtime split:

StageWhat's caught
Plugin author build (cargo build)Plugin SDK type-state + #[capabilities(...)] macro: capability declarations match plugin code surface.
tau installPlugin manifest ↔ embedded CAPABILITIES constant disagreement (cross-check).
tau resolve / tau installDependency resolution (priority 5) — version conflicts, unreachable sources.
tau check (future sub-project A)Static validation: every capability shape in every plugin must be supported by the target adapter. Project tau.toml's tier requests must respect deployment floors. Capability override (compute_effective) must produce a non-empty grant.
tau build --target <triple> (future sub-project C)All the above + bundle production with content hash.
tau run startupAdapter probe + final cross-check (Layer 3 of priority 12's design). On a match against a previously-built bundle, this is a fast verification rather than a re-derivation.
Plugin runtimeOS sandbox enforcement (Layer 4 of priority 12's design). Catches plugin-vs-declaration drift.

The first six stages eliminate ENTIRE CLASSES of failures from runtime. The seventh exists for defense-in-depth: even if every prior layer was correct, a buggy or compromised plugin can still attempt a violation at runtime; the OS sandbox is the final backstop.

Why this matters

Common ground truth

A team writing a tau program can hand it off — to a colleague's laptop, to CI, to a production server — with a single, verifiable guarantee: if this bundle was built for this target, it will run on this target. No "works on my machine" surprises. No silent sandbox degradation. No "but on CI it crashes because seccomp blocked something."

This is the same guarantee Rust gives developers and the same guarantee Docker gives operators, applied to the agent-workflow domain.

Defensive composition

Agent workflows compose human prompts, LLM completions, and tool calls. The composition is inherently uncertain (an LLM might request anything). What CAN be made certain is the boundary: the set of operations a tool is permitted to perform on the user's machine. By making that boundary a compile-time property, we push the uncertainty surface where it belongs (the LLM's output) and out of where it doesn't (the system's enforcement).

Distribution

A tau application bundle is a single artifact you can sign, publish, audit, and verify. The "tau equivalent of a Rust binary or Docker image" is a real workflow: compile your agent workflow once, distribute the bundle, run it anywhere a matching target exists.

Realization roadmap

Each item below is a future sub-project with its own ADR. Order is tentative; some can run in parallel.

Sub-project A — tau check subcommand

Static validation as a standalone CLI verb. Reuses priority 12's Layer 3 logic but invocable independently of tau run. Target use cases: CI gates ("does this branch's tau.toml validate?"), IDE extensions, pre-commit hooks. Estimated scope: ~3 weeks.

Sub-project B — Tau target triple registry

Formal naming convention + registry of supported targets, each with a documented capability matrix (which CapabilityShape variants the target enforces). New triples land via ADR amendments to a "target registry" ADR. Estimated scope: ~2 weeks (mostly documentation + naming).

Sub-project C — tau build --target <triple> subcommand

Produces the deployment artifact. Bundle format design + content hashing + reproducibility verification. Builds on priority 7's tree_hash and verify primitives. tau run --bundle <file> executes a bundle. Estimated scope: ~6 weeks.

Sub-project D — Capability vocabulary forward-compatibility

Versioning + stability guarantees for CapabilityShape. A bundle compiled against tau v1.2's capability shapes must continue to run on tau v1.3+ with new shapes added. Stability discipline + ADR amendments. Estimated scope: ~2 weeks (heavy on ADR, light on code).

Sub-project E — Cross-machine reproducibility verification

Extends tau verify (priority 7) so a deployed bundle on a target machine can be verified to match the bundle the project author built. Detects bundle tampering between build and run. Estimated scope: ~3 weeks.

Sub-project F — Remote target backends

Vercel Sandbox, Sandcastle, generic remote-execution providers. Each is an additional Sandbox impl. Major design concerns: authentication, IPC channel networking, cold-start latency budgets. Estimated scope per backend: ~4-6 weeks.

Sub-project G — WASM target backend

Most ambitious. Plugins compile to wasm32-wasip2. The tau-plugin-sdk migrates to support the new ABI. Plugin distribution becomes .wasm artifacts. Plausibly a Phase 2 effort in its own right. Estimated scope: ~12+ weeks.

What this is NOT

  • Not a programming language in the classical sense. There is no tau-source-file parsed by a tau-frontend that generates code. The "language" is the configuration grammar of tau.toml plus the runtime semantics of the agent loop. "Compilation" is validation + lockfile + bundle production, not code generation.
  • Not a replacement for Rust. Plugins are still written in Rust (today; potentially WASI-targeted Rust via WASM target later). Tau is the build system + runtime + sandbox layer ABOVE plugins, not the language they're written in.
  • Not a marketplace or registry. Distribution mechanisms are separate concerns. A tau bundle could be distributed via git, http, OCI registry, or any other mechanism; tau the toolchain is agnostic.
  • Not a v1.0 commitment. This document is a vision; individual elements may shift as sub-projects deliver. The current Phase-1-and-Tier-3 work establishes the foundations; Phase 2 (as outlined above) realizes the vision.

  • ADR-0013 — REPL persistence, hexagonal session storage.

  • crates/tau-pkg/src/install.rs — install pipeline.
  • crates/tau-pkg/src/resolve.rs — dependency resolution.
  • crates/tau-pkg/src/tree_hash.rs — content-hashing primitive (verify foundation).
  • crates/tau-pkg/src/verify.rs — verify primitive (bundle verification foundation).
  • crates/tau-runtime/src/stream.rs — streaming pump (bundle execution foundation).

Why two-layer skills

A tau skill is two files in a directory: SKILL.md (the prompt) and tau.toml (the manifest). This isn't an obvious choice — most agent frameworks embed both in a single file. This page explains why tau picked the two-layer split, what trade-offs it locked in, and how it interacts with the broader Anthropic Agent Skills ecosystem.

For the surface details, see Reference: skill manifest schema. For the design history, see ADR-0025.

The reframing that produced Option D

When Skills-1 was designed, four shapes were on the table:

  • A: Typed [skill] block in tau.toml, with the system prompt embedded as a TOML triple-quoted string.
  • B: External SKILL.md referenced by relative path from tau.toml, with a tau-specific filename convention.
  • C: Adopt the Agent Skills spec format verbatim (just SKILL.md, no tau.toml — drop tau-specific packaging).
  • D: Two-layer: Anthropic-compatible SKILL.md (frontmatter + body) PLUS tau.toml for packaging metadata + capabilities.

Option D won. The reframing: Anthropic's skill format already defines the prompt layout we want. We don't need to compete with it on prompt encoding; we need to extend it with packaging.

The result is a skill directory that's simultaneously:

  • A valid Anthropic Agent Skill (just remove tau.toml to ship to claude-code or another Anthropic consumer).
  • A tau package with capabilities, semver, dependencies, lockfile participation.

What each layer owns

flowchart LR
    subgraph A["Anthropic Agent Skills format"]
        SK["SKILL.md<br/><i>prompt content</i><br/>+ YAML frontmatter (name, description)"]
    end
    subgraph T["tau-specific layer"]
        TT["tau.toml<br/><i>typed package metadata</i><br/>kind = &quot;skill&quot;<br/>[[capabilities]]<br/>[skill.requires_skills]<br/>version, source, authors"]
    end
    SK -.->|name<br/>must match| TT
    A -->|<code>tau install</code>| INSTALLED[("installed skill<br/>SKILL.md + tau.toml<br/>+ lockfile entry")]
    T -->|<code>tau install</code>| INSTALLED
    INSTALLED -->|"<code>tau skill export</code>"| OUT["SKILL.md only<br/>(capabilities dropped<br/>with warning)"]
    OUT -.->|byte-identical iff<br/>capabilities = [] and<br/>no requires_skills| SK

SKILL.md owns: the prompt content.

  • The YAML frontmatter declares name and description (both required, both validated).
  • The Markdown body is the system prompt. It becomes the spawned child agent's system_prompt at runtime (per ADR-0028).
  • Format is fixed by the Anthropic Agent Skills spec; tau doesn't extend it.

tau.toml owns: everything else.

  • Package identity: name, version, source, authors.
  • Capability declarations ([[capabilities]] blocks).
  • Sub-skill dependencies ([skill.requires_skills]).
  • Package kind (kind = "skill" — disambiguates from kind = "tool" / kind = "llm-backend").

The two layers must agree on name (validated on install). Everything else is independent: tau can add [skill.requires_tools], requires_skills, future fields, without touching SKILL.md.

The roundtrip claim

For any tau skill with capabilities = [] and no requires_skills:

tau install <source> → tau skill export <name> → re-install

produces an identical SKILL.md byte-for-byte (and the same name + description). Skills-5 ships this as tests/skill_format_roundtrip.rs.

For capability-bearing skills, the export is one-way (Anthropic format doesn't preserve capabilities). tau skill export --strict makes the metadata-drop a hard error if round-trippability matters.

The roundtrip claim is what makes the two-layer architecture worth it: tau can extend the Anthropic format without forking it.

What this rules out

Two consequences worth being explicit about:

1. We can't bake tau-specific behavior into SKILL.md. No tau- specific YAML frontmatter extensions (e.g., x-tau-capabilities), no tau-flavored Markdown syntax. Why: any such extension would break the byte-identical roundtrip claim for pure-prompt skills, and the Anthropic ecosystem ignores unknown YAML keys anyway so there's no benefit.

Skills-5 explicitly rejected an x-tau-capabilities YAML extension for this reason.

2. We can't combine tau.toml and SKILL.md into a single file. A skill is fundamentally a directory of files. The ${SKILL_DIR}/... substitution + multi-file payloads (e.g., references/ subdirs) depend on the directory being the unit of distribution. Embedding everything in tau.toml would lose that.

Comparison with neighboring systems

SystemPrompt encodingPackaging
Anthropic Agent Skills (vanilla)SKILL.md (YAML + Markdown)none (just the directory)
tauSKILL.md (same)tau.toml (additive)
Single-file agents (e.g., .prompt files)one TOML / YAML / JSON filenone
Plugin-style (Python decorators, MCP servers)code-embeddedlanguage-native imports

tau differs from each:

  • vs Anthropic vanilla: gains semver, capabilities, lockfile.
  • vs single-file: gains multi-file payloads, structured packaging.
  • vs plugin-style: gains LLM-readable prompts (skills aren't code).

Sub-skill composition (currently advisory)

The [skill.requires_skills] block lets a skill declare that it's meant to be used with another skill:

[[skill.requires_skills]]
name = "fact-checker"
version_req = "^0.1"

In tau v1, this is advisory. The runtime doesn't:

  • Auto-install dependencies on tau install.
  • Auto-spawn sub-skills when the parent is invoked.
  • Enforce that the dependency is present at spawn time.

It documents the relationship for users authoring agents (so they can install the dependencies + grant the parent skill skill.spawn authorization for them).

A future Skills sub-project may tighten this if a concrete use case emerges.

When NOT to write a skill

A skill is the right shape when:

  • The work is purely prompt-driven (LLM reads input → emits output).
  • The skill has clear boundaries (one purpose, one entry point).
  • The capability set is small (one or two fs / process / net access).

A skill is the WRONG shape when:

  • The work requires real code (compute, parsing, custom logic). Use a tool plugin instead (kind = "tool").
  • The work requires many capabilities or long chains of tool calls. Compose smaller skills + tools instead.
  • The work doesn't generalize beyond a single project. Just inline the prompt in your agent definition.

The skill abstraction earns its keep when it's installable, named, versioned, and reusable across agents.

Further reading

  • ADR-0025: foundation
  • ADR-0028: runtime invocation
  • ADR-0029: Anthropic interop
  • Tutorial: Build your first skill
  • Reference: Skill manifest schema

Architecture Decision Records (ADRs)

This directory holds tau's ADRs. Each ADR is a numbered Markdown file (NNNN-title.md) recording one decision in MADR style.

When an ADR is required

Per QG18, ADRs are required for:

  • Changes to project guidelines (anything in CONSTITUTION.md).
  • Additions to or breaking changes in public APIs (tau-runtime exports, serve-mode IPC schema).
  • Changes to the serve-mode protocol.
  • Changes to the package manifest format.
  • Changes to plugin trait boundaries.

Other changes (bugfixes, refactors within a crate, docs updates) do not require ADRs and are recorded in commit messages and PR discussion (PG3).

Filing an ADR

  1. Copy template.md to NNNN-<short-title>.md where NNNN is one greater than the highest existing ADR number.
  2. Fill in Context, Decision, Consequences, and Alternatives.
  3. Open a PR. The ADR's status starts as Proposed.
  4. The maintainer reviews. On acceptance, status changes to Accepted and the PR is merged.
  5. Per the Constitution §4 amendment process, guideline-changing ADRs wait at least 24 hours between draft and merge.

Index

ADRTitleStatus
0001Bootstrap decisionsAccepted
0002Manifest format, capability evolution, escape-hatch policyAccepted
0003tau-ports trait surfaceAccepted
0004tau-pkg package manager — public API, storage layout, lockfileAccepted
0005Custom serde for PackageSource and PackageKindAccepted
0006tau-runtime kernel + Tool capabilities amendmentAccepted
0007tau-cli + tau-runtime amendments (capability filter, run_with_history)Accepted
0008Plugin loading mechanism — IPC over MessagePack-RPC + tau-pkg, tau-runtime, tau-domain amendmentsAccepted

"Easier" or "preference" is not a reason — name the trade-off.></p> <div style="break-before: page; page-break-before: always;"></div><h1 id="adr-0001-bootstrap-decisions"><a class="header" href="#adr-0001-bootstrap-decisions">ADR-0001: Bootstrap decisions</a></h1> <p><strong>Status:</strong> Accepted <strong>Date:</strong> 2026-04-24 <strong>Deciders:</strong> Titouan Lebocq</p> <h2 id="context-1"><a class="header" href="#context-1">Context</a></h2> <p>Tau begins as an empty repository. The first commits make several decisions that pin the implementation of constitutional guidelines — license selection (QG10), MSRV value (QG7), workspace shape (Constitution §1 Crate scope), CI provider (QG6), governance file content (QG10), and documentation framework (QG8). PG3 ("decisions in commit messages") is insufficient for these because future ADRs must amend them by reference, and commit messages are not stable referents.</p> <p>This ADR records the bootstrap state so future ADRs can supersede specific items without ambiguity.</p> <h2 id="decision-1"><a class="header" href="#decision-1">Decision</a></h2> <ol> <li> <p><strong>License: <code>MIT OR Apache-2.0</code>.</strong> Dual-licensed via <code>LICENSE-MIT</code> and <code>LICENSE-APACHE</code> at repo root; declared in <code>[workspace.package].license</code>.</p> </li> <li> <p><strong>MSRV: <code>1.91</code>.</strong> Pinned in <code>[workspace.package].rust-version</code>. Verified by the MSRV row of the CI test matrix. Bumped by minor-version change only (QG7); never silently in patch releases (QG11).</p> </li> <li> <p><strong>Workspace shape: 8 crates, all scaffolded empty.</strong> <code>tau-domain</code>, <code>tau-ports</code>, <code>tau-infra</code>, <code>tau-app</code>, <code>tau-pkg</code>, <code>tau-observe</code>, <code>tau-runtime</code>, <code>tau-cli</code>. Members listed explicitly in root <code>Cargo.toml</code> (no glob).</p> </li> <li> <p><strong>CI: GitHub Actions, 3-OS × 2-toolchain matrix.</strong> Linux + macOS + Windows × stable + 1.91. Windows is non-blocking (<code>continue-on-error</code>) per G15. Three jobs: <code>fmt</code>, <code>clippy</code>, <code>test</code>. No caching, no <code>cargo audit</code>/<code>cargo-deny</code>, no coverage in Plan 1 — Phase 2 additions per QG14/QG16.</p> </li> <li> <p><strong>Conventional Commits enforced by docs only.</strong> <code>CONTRIBUTING.md</code> describes the format. No <code>commitlint</code> or git hooks in Plan 1.</p> </li> <li> <p><strong>Toolchain: <code>rust-toolchain.toml</code> pins <code>stable</code> for local dev.</strong> CI overrides per matrix entry via <code>dtolnay/rust-toolchain</code> action.</p> </li> <li> <p><strong>Docs framework: Diátaxis.</strong> Per QG8. Sections under <code>docs/</code>: <code>tutorials/</code>, <code>how-to/</code>, <code>reference/</code>, <code>explanation/</code>, <code>decisions/</code>.</p> </li> <li> <p><strong>Process artifacts under <code>docs/superpowers/</code>.</strong> Specs (from the brainstorming skill) and plans (from the writing-plans skill) are kept in-repo so reviewers see how decisions were made. Not part of the published Diátaxis tree.</p> </li> <li> <p><strong>Governance files: README, LICENSE-{MIT,APACHE}, CONTRIBUTING, SECURITY, CODE_OF_CONDUCT, GOVERNANCE, ROADMAP, CHANGELOG</strong> at repo root, all present from the bootstrap commit. Satisfies QG10 in full.</p> </li> <li> <p><strong>Lint denials enabled per-crate from day one.</strong> <code>#![forbid(unsafe_code)]</code> (QG4), <code>#![deny(missing_docs)]</code> (QG9), <code>#![deny(rustdoc::broken_intra_doc_links)]</code> (QG9) on every library <code>lib.rs</code>. Binary (<code>tau-cli/main.rs</code>) carries <code>forbid(unsafe_code)</code> only; its public surface is the CLI, not Rust items.</p> </li> </ol> <h2 id="consequences-1"><a class="header" href="#consequences-1">Consequences</a></h2> <p><strong>Positive:</strong></p> <ul> <li>Future contributors land code, not infrastructure. Every workspace-level question is decided.</li> <li>Lint denials caught at first commit; no retro-enforcement campaign needed.</li> <li>The MSRV CI row catches accidental MSRV drift before merge.</li> <li>Diátaxis structure is in place; new docs have an obvious home.</li> </ul> <p><strong>Negative:</strong></p> <ul> <li>Eight empty crates inflate the repo before any code lands. Acceptable — retrofitting a workspace member is a larger diff than landing one upfront, and the constitution names all eight.</li> <li>Dual licensing requires every contributor to be aware of the MIT-or-Apache choice; documented in <code>CONTRIBUTING.md</code>.</li> </ul> <p><strong>Neutral:</strong></p> <ul> <li>Phase 2 will add <code>cargo audit</code> and <code>cargo-deny</code> (QG16), <code>actions/cache</code> for CI speed, and likely commit signing. Each is its own ADR when added.</li> </ul> <h2 id="alternatives-considered-1"><a class="header" href="#alternatives-considered-1">Alternatives considered</a></h2> <ul> <li><strong>License: Apache-2.0 only.</strong> Loses MIT compatibility with old GPL-2.0 consumers; rejected for a runtime tool that may be embedded in permissively-licensed downstream projects.</li> <li><strong>License: MIT only.</strong> No explicit patent grant; rejected because tau has plugin traits that may attract patent claims as the ecosystem matures.</li> <li><strong>Workspace: defer empty crates until first content lands.</strong> Rejected because retrofitting a workspace member is more diff than landing one empty (PR-noise economics).</li> <li><strong>CI: GitLab CI or sourcehut builds.</strong> Rejected because the repo is hosted on GitHub; cross-host CI adds tooling without value.</li> <li><strong>Commitlint / Husky for Conventional Commits.</strong> Rejected because it adds a Node toolchain dependency to a Rust repo for a solo-maintainer project. Revisit if non-conforming commits accumulate (QG23-style reactive enforcement).</li> <li><strong>Skip ADR-0001; record decisions in commit messages only.</strong> Rejected per QG18; bootstrap pins guideline implementations that need a stable referent for amendment.</li> </ul> <div style="break-before: page; page-break-before: always;"></div><h1 id="adr-0002-manifest-format-capability-evolution-and-escape-hatch-policy"><a class="header" href="#adr-0002-manifest-format-capability-evolution-and-escape-hatch-policy">ADR-0002: Manifest format, capability evolution, and escape-hatch policy</a></h1> <p><strong>Status:</strong> Accepted <strong>Date:</strong> 2026-04-26 <strong>Supersedes:</strong> —</p> <h2 id="context-2"><a class="header" href="#context-2">Context</a></h2> <p>tau-domain (sub-project 1) introduces the package manifest type (<code>PackageManifest</code>), a hierarchical capability enum (<code>Capability</code>), and four escape-hatch variants (<code>Capability::Custom</code>, <code>MessagePayload::Custom</code>, <code>PackageKind::Custom</code>, <code>FailureKind::InternalError</code>).</p> <p>Per QG18, public-API additions to data shapes that downstream plugins will deserialize require an ADR. This ADR records the v0.1 manifest field set, the rules under which the <code>Capability</code> typed enum evolves, and the policy governing all escape-hatch variants in tau core.</p> <h2 id="decision-2"><a class="header" href="#decision-2">Decision</a></h2> <h3 id="1-manifest-field-set-v01"><a class="header" href="#1-manifest-field-set-v01">1. Manifest field set (v0.1)</a></h3> <p>The v0.1 <code>UncheckedManifest</code> / <code>PackageManifest</code> carries:</p> <ul> <li><code>name: PackageName</code></li> <li><code>version: semver::Version</code></li> <li><code>description: String</code> (non-empty; enforced by <code>validate()</code>)</li> <li><code>authors: Vec<String></code></li> <li><code>license: Option<String></code> (SPDX expression as opaque text)</li> <li><code>source: PackageSource</code></li> <li><code>kind: PackageKind</code></li> <li><code>dependencies: Vec<PackageDep></code></li> <li><code>capabilities: Vec<Capability></code></li> </ul> <p>Adding fields is a non-breaking minor (the struct is <code>#[non_exhaustive]</code>). Removing or renaming is a breaking minor pre-1.0 per QG11.</p> <h3 id="2-hierarchical-capability-shape"><a class="header" href="#2-hierarchical-capability-shape">2. Hierarchical Capability shape</a></h3> <p><code>Capability</code> is a top-level <code>#[non_exhaustive]</code> enum with five variants: <code>Filesystem(FsCapability)</code>, <code>Network(NetCapability)</code>, <code>Process(ProcessCapability)</code>, <code>Agent(AgentCapability)</code>, and <code>Custom { name, params }</code>. Each per-namespace sub-enum is also <code>#[non_exhaustive]</code>, and each variant within (e.g. <code>FsCapability::Read</code>) is <code>#[non_exhaustive]</code> so additive field evolution is non-breaking.</p> <h3 id="3-canonicalization-at-deserialization"><a class="header" href="#3-canonicalization-at-deserialization">3. Canonicalization at deserialization</a></h3> <p>Manifest TOML uses the flat dot-namespaced form:</p> <pre><code class="language-toml">[[capabilities]] kind = "fs.read" paths = ["${PROJECT}/**"] </code></pre> <p>A custom <code>Deserialize</code> impl on <code>Capability</code> maps recognized <code>kind</code> strings (<code>"fs.read"</code>, <code>"fs.write"</code>, <code>"net.http"</code>, <code>"process.spawn"</code>, <code>"agent.spawn"</code>, etc.) onto the variant tree. Unknown <code>kind</code> values fall through to <code>Capability::Custom { name, params }</code>. New typed variants in v0.X auto-promote existing manifests via the same canonicalization — plugin authors do not need to update manifests when typed variants land.</p> <p>A consequence of canonicalization-at-deserialization is that the shadowed-name case round-trips into the typed variant: a <code>Capability::Custom { name: "fs.read", params: ... }</code> constructed in Rust serializes to <code>kind = "fs.read"</code> and re-deserializes as the typed <code>Capability::Filesystem(FsCapability::Read { ... })</code>. This is intentional and supports the v0.X auto-promotion path above. Reserved typed param names (<code>paths</code>, <code>max_bytes</code>, <code>hosts</code>, <code>methods</code>, <code>commands</code>, <code>allowed_kinds</code>) should therefore not be reused inside <code>Capability::Custom.params</code> for unrelated meanings.</p> <h3 id="4-naming-convention"><a class="header" href="#4-naming-convention">4. Naming convention</a></h3> <p>The dot-namespaced <code><domain>.<verb></code> convention (e.g. <code>fs.read</code>, <code>net.http</code>) is <strong>recommended, not mandated</strong>. tau-domain validates only "non-empty" on <code>Capability::Custom.name</code>. Plugin authors who want a non-conforming name (e.g. <code>myorg/special-cap</code>) may use it via <code>Custom</code>.</p> <h3 id="5-escape-hatch-policy"><a class="header" href="#5-escape-hatch-policy">5. Escape-hatch policy</a></h3> <p>Prefer typed variants for known shapes; allow <code>Custom</code> / <code>InternalError</code> escape hatches with documented rationale. <strong>Every escape hatch in tau core is tracked in <code>docs/explanation/escape-hatches.md</code></strong> with location, reason, promotion trigger, and status. PRs that introduce, promote, or remove an escape hatch update the registry in the same commit. Each escape-hatch variant's rustdoc carries a link to its registry anchor.</p> <p>This applies uniformly to:</p> <ul> <li><code>Capability::Custom</code></li> <li><code>MessagePayload::Custom</code></li> <li><code>PackageKind::Custom</code></li> <li><code>FailureKind::InternalError</code></li> <li>any future escape hatches added in tau core.</li> </ul> <h3 id="6-required-llm_backend"><a class="header" href="#6-required-llm_backend">6. Required <code>llm_backend</code></a></h3> <p><code>AgentDefinition.llm_backend</code> is <code>PackageName</code> (non-optional) at v0.1. Rationale: Constitution Appendix C explicitly defines an agent as a process with an LLM backend; G4 reinforces. If a non-LLM agent use case materializes (sub-project 4+), <code>llm_backend</code> becomes <code>Option<PackageName></code> via a pre-1.0 minor breaking change.</p> <h3 id="7-mechanical-enforcement-of-the-registry"><a class="header" href="#7-mechanical-enforcement-of-the-registry">7. Mechanical enforcement of the registry</a></h3> <p>A CI-blocking integration test (<code>crates/tau-domain/tests/escape_hatch_registry.rs</code>) scans every <code>.rs</code> file in the workspace for variants named <code>Custom</code> or <code>InternalError</code>, parses their rustdoc for a link to the registry, and verifies each anchor exists in <code>docs/explanation/escape-hatches.md</code>. Stale registry entries also fail the test. Combined with the PR template checkbox and the rustdoc convention, this enforces decision 5 in three layers: documentation (CONTRIBUTING.md + rustdoc), PR-time prompt (template), and CI gate (test).</p> <h3 id="8-valuebytes-wire-format"><a class="header" href="#8-valuebytes-wire-format">8. <code>Value::Bytes</code> wire format</a></h3> <p><code>Value::Bytes(Vec<u8>)</code> (the message-payload byte-vector variant) is serialized in human-readable formats (TOML, JSON) as a base64 string prefixed with the reserved sentinel <code>@bytes:</code>. The prefix lets the custom <code>Deserialize</code> distinguish a byte-payload string from an ordinary <code>Value::String</code>. Consequently, <code>@bytes:</code> is a reserved prefix on <code>Value::String</code> — strings starting with <code>@bytes:</code> are not representable as <code>Value::String</code> and must use <code>Value::Bytes</code>. Binary formats (e.g. bincode) carry bytes natively and ignore this convention.</p> <h2 id="consequences-2"><a class="header" href="#consequences-2">Consequences</a></h2> <ul> <li>The wire format for <code>Capability</code> is committed at v0.1: flat <code>kind = "<dot.namespaced>"</code> form with sibling field-shaped params. Deviating from this form is a breaking change.</li> <li>Plugin authors need to know the canonical names (<code>fs.read</code>, etc.) to produce manifests that map to typed variants; they don't <em>have</em> to use them — <code>Custom</code> always works.</li> <li>The escape-hatch registry is a living document; every PR that introduces or modifies an escape hatch touches it.</li> <li>The CI registry test depends on the rustdoc convention of linking to <code>escape-hatches.md#<anchor></code>. Variants that violate this fail CI.</li> <li>Adding new typed <code>FailureKind</code> variants is non-breaking (the enum is <code>#[non_exhaustive]</code>); demoting <code>InternalError</code> to a typed kind is similarly additive.</li> <li>Required <code>llm_backend</code> rules out non-LLM agents at v0.1; we accept the migration cost if and when that case appears.</li> </ul> <h2 id="alternatives-considered-2"><a class="header" href="#alternatives-considered-2">Alternatives considered</a></h2> <ul> <li><strong>String-only capability dispatch</strong> (rejected at brainstorm). Forces every consumer to write <code>match name.as_str()</code> boilerplate; gives up type safety for no v0.1 benefit.</li> <li><strong>Flat <code>Capability</code> enum without namespace structure</strong> (α; rejected in favor of β). Would have been more compact at v0.1 but β anticipates per-namespace enforcement layers in tau-runtime.</li> <li><strong>No escape-hatch registry</strong>, just rustdoc warnings (rejected). Aspirational rules without mechanical enforcement decay quickly.</li> <li><strong>Top-level <code>DomainError</code> umbrella</strong> (rejected at brainstorm Q5). Per-concern enums forever; consumers wanting "any tau-domain error" wrap their own.</li> <li><strong><code>Option<PackageName></code> for <code>llm_backend</code> from day one</strong> (rejected). Adds permanent ceremony at every reader for a case that may not materialize; pre-1.0 SemVer makes the loosen-later path cheap.</li> </ul> <div style="break-before: page; page-break-before: always;"></div><h1 id="adr-0003-tau-ports-trait-surface"><a class="header" href="#adr-0003-tau-ports-trait-surface">ADR-0003: tau-ports trait surface</a></h1> <p><strong>Status:</strong> Accepted <strong>Date:</strong> 2026-04-26 <strong>Supersedes:</strong> —</p> <h2 id="context-3"><a class="header" href="#context-3">Context</a></h2> <p>tau-ports (sub-project 2) defines the four plugin trait boundaries that every tau plugin author programs against: <code>LlmBackend</code>, <code>Tool</code>, <code>Storage</code>, and <code>Sandbox</code>. These traits, together with their request/response data shapes, are the most permanent surface in the workspace — once a plugin ecosystem starts, breaking them invalidates every out-of-tree plugin.</p> <p>Per QG18, plugin trait boundaries require an ADR. Per Constitution §1 crate scope, tau-ports is <code>no_std</code>-leaning (currently <code>std</code>-only but runtime-agnostic) and depends only on <code>tau-domain</code> plus <code>thiserror</code>. This ADR records the decisions taken across spec §3 (the type-by-type design) and locks them at v0.1: async story, trait shapes, error policy, serde stance, escape-hatch registration, sandbox provisionality, and where mocks live.</p> <h2 id="decision-3"><a class="header" href="#decision-3">Decision</a></h2> <h3 id="1-native-async-fn-in-trait"><a class="header" href="#1-native-async-fn-in-trait">1. Native <code>async fn in trait</code></a></h3> <p>tau-ports uses native <code>async fn in trait</code> (stabilized in Rust 1.75, covered by MSRV 1.91 from ADR-0001) for every async trait method. The <code>async-trait</code> macro is not used; a sync-only API is rejected. Each public trait carries <code>#[allow(async_fn_in_trait)]</code> to suppress the "may have undesirable side-effects" clippy warning, since tau-ports does not require <code>Send</code> futures at the trait level — runtimes that need <code>Send</code> impose it at the bound site.</p> <p>The crate stays runtime-agnostic: no <code>tokio</code>, <code>async-std</code>, or <code>futures-executor</code> dependency. Returned futures are <code>impl Future</code> in trait position, bound by the trait's lifetime parameters.</p> <h3 id="2-four-trait-shapes"><a class="header" href="#2-four-trait-shapes">2. Four trait shapes</a></h3> <h4 id="llmbackend"><a class="header" href="#llmbackend"><code>LlmBackend</code></a></h4> <ul> <li>Two methods: <code>complete(&self, req: CompletionRequest) -> Result<CompletionResponse, LlmError></code> and <code>stream(&self, req: CompletionRequest) -> Result<CompletionStream, LlmError></code>.</li> <li>A blanket helper provides "implement one, get the other" via the <code>BackendCompletionExt</code> extension trait (drains a stream into a response; or upgrades a <code>complete</code> to a one-shot stream).</li> <li>Messages are multi-block: <code>LlmProviderMessage</code> carries <code>Vec<LlmContentBlock></code> so a single assistant turn can mix text, tool-use, and tool-result blocks.</li> <li>Streaming chunks for <code>tool_use</code> are plugin-buffered: the backend accumulates the JSON until the block closes, then emits one <code>StreamEvent::ToolUse</code> rather than partial-JSON chunks. Consumers do not see incomplete tool calls.</li> </ul> <h4 id="tool"><a class="header" href="#tool"><code>Tool</code></a></h4> <ul> <li>Stateful: associated type <code>Session: Send + 'static</code>, with <code>init(&self) -> Result<Session, ToolError></code>, <code>invoke(&self, session: &mut Session, args: Value) -> Result<ToolResult, ToolError></code>, and <code>teardown(&self, session: Session) -> Result<(), ToolError></code>.</li> <li>Stateless tools use a <code>StatelessAdapter<F></code> newtype that implements <code>Tool</code> with <code>Session = ()</code>; authors write a single async closure.</li> <li>Dual error model: <code>Result<ToolResult, ToolError></code>, where <code>ToolResult</code> itself carries an <code>is_error: bool</code> flag. Hard errors (the tool itself failed to execute) return <code>Err(ToolError)</code>; soft errors (the tool ran but the model should see a failure message) return <code>Ok(ToolResult { is_error: true, .. })</code>.</li> </ul> <h4 id="storage"><a class="header" href="#storage"><code>Storage</code></a></h4> <ul> <li>KV-only at v0.1: <code>get</code>, <code>put</code>, <code>delete</code>, <code>list_prefix</code>. No transactions, no TTL, no watch/subscribe.</li> <li>Typed newtypes <code>Namespace(String)</code> and <code>Key(String)</code> prevent string-typing collisions and namespace forgery; both validate non-empty and reject <code>\0</code> on construction.</li> <li>Values are <code>Vec<u8></code>. Serialization is the caller's responsibility.</li> </ul> <h4 id="sandbox"><a class="header" href="#sandbox"><code>Sandbox</code></a></h4> <ul> <li>Provisional stub at v0.1 (see decision 6).</li> <li>Opaque <code>Handle</code> newtype: implementations may park a PID, container id, or VM handle behind it; consumers may only <code>wait</code>, <code>kill</code>, or query status.</li> <li><code>ResourceLimits</code> is four-axis: <code>cpu_seconds</code>, <code>memory_bytes</code>, <code>file_descriptors</code>, <code>wall_clock</code>. The wall-clock axis is belt-and-suspenders against runaway sandboxed work.</li> <li><code>WorkingContext</code> (cwd, env, stdin handle) is optional on <code>spawn</code> — some sandbox kinds (e.g. WASI) do not honor a host cwd.</li> </ul> <h3 id="3-error-policy"><a class="header" href="#3-error-policy">3. Error policy</a></h3> <p>Each trait has its own concrete error enum: <code>LlmError</code>, <code>ToolError</code>, <code>StorageError</code>, <code>SandboxError</code>. There is no top-level <code>PortsError</code> umbrella.</p> <p><code>ToolError</code> composes the others via <code>#[from]</code> conversions: <code>ToolError::Llm(LlmError)</code> and <code>ToolError::Storage(StorageError)</code>. Tools that delegate to a backend or read from storage propagate errors with <code>?</code> without manual mapping.</p> <p>Every error enum exposes <code>is_retryable(&self) -> bool</code> — a <em>heuristic</em> predicate for transient-vs-permanent classification (rate limits, transient network, broken pipe → true; bad request, schema error, missing key → false). Consumers may build retry loops on it but should not treat the answer as authoritative.</p> <p>Each enum carries an <code>Internal { source: anyhow::Error }</code> (or <code>InternalError</code>) variant as a typed escape hatch. Per ADR-0002 decision 5, every <code>Internal*</code> variant is registered in <code>docs/explanation/escape-hatches.md</code> with reason and promotion trigger; the CI registry test enforces this.</p> <h3 id="4-no-serde-feature-at-v01"><a class="header" href="#4-no-serde-feature-at-v01">4. No serde feature at v0.1</a></h3> <p>tau-ports does not expose a <code>serde</code> feature on any of its types. Plugin trait shapes are in-process Rust values; (de)serialization is unnecessary for v0.1 dispatch.</p> <p>The trigger for adding a <code>serde</code> feature is the first cross-process consumer: either a Phase-1 RPC sandbox kind (where requests/responses cross a process boundary) or the eventual out-of-process plugin model. Adding the feature is non-breaking (gated behind <code>#[cfg(feature = "serde")]</code>).</p> <h3 id="5-provider_specific-escape-hatch-on-completionrequest"><a class="header" href="#5-provider_specific-escape-hatch-on-completionrequest">5. <code>provider_specific</code> escape hatch on <code>CompletionRequest</code></a></h3> <p><code>CompletionRequest</code> carries a <code>provider_specific: BTreeMap<String, Value></code> field for vendor-specific parameters that have no typed equivalent (e.g. Anthropic-only <code>extended_thinking_budget_tokens</code>, OpenAI-only <code>seed</code>). The <code>BTreeMap</code> deterministic ordering matters for snapshot tests and idempotent retry signatures.</p> <p>This is a registered escape hatch: it lives in <code>docs/explanation/escape-hatches.md</code> with the <strong>promotion rule</strong> that any key appearing in two or more independent plugins is promoted to a typed field on <code>CompletionRequest</code> (or a typed sub-struct under it) in the next minor.</p> <h3 id="6-sandbox-provisional-caveat"><a class="header" href="#6-sandbox-provisional-caveat">6. Sandbox provisional caveat</a></h3> <p>The v0.1 <code>Sandbox</code> trait is a <strong>provisional sketch</strong>. Real sandboxing work (Linux namespaces, seccomp, WASI capabilities, Firecracker VM, or the eventual choice) lives in Phase 1+ and will likely require shape changes — additional methods, different <code>Handle</code> semantics, or splitting <code>Sandbox</code> into multiple traits per kind.</p> <p>SemVer is <strong>disclaimed</strong> for the <code>Sandbox</code> trait beyond the major-version bump that introduces production sandboxing. The trait's rustdoc carries a "Stability: provisional" notice. Plugin authors targeting v0.1 sandbox may need to rewrite at the major bump.</p> <p>The other three traits (<code>LlmBackend</code>, <code>Tool</code>, <code>Storage</code>) are not provisional; they follow normal pre-1.0 SemVer (QG11).</p> <h3 id="7-mocks-live-in-tau-ports-under-test-fixtures"><a class="header" href="#7-mocks-live-in-tau-ports-under-test-fixtures">7. Mocks live in tau-ports under <code>test-fixtures</code></a></h3> <p>Test fixtures (<code>MockLlmBackend</code>, <code>MockTool</code>, <code>MockStorage</code>, <code>MockSandbox</code>) live in tau-ports itself, gated behind a <code>test-fixtures</code> cargo feature. Consumer crates depend on tau-ports with <code>features = ["test-fixtures"]</code> in their <code>[dev-dependencies]</code>.</p> <p>Alternatives — per-consumer mocks or a separate <code>tau-ports-test</code> crate — were rejected. Per-consumer duplicates the same mock five times across the workspace and drifts. A separate crate adds a publish target and a SemVer surface for what is conceptually one trait surface; the cargo-feature pattern colocates the mock with the trait it mocks, so a trait change updates the mock in the same diff.</p> <h2 id="consequences-3"><a class="header" href="#consequences-3">Consequences</a></h2> <ul> <li>The trait shapes and request/response types are the v0.1 public surface for plugin authors. Adding methods is a breaking minor pre-1.0 (QG11); adding fields to <code>#[non_exhaustive]</code> request/response types is non-breaking.</li> <li><code>async fn in trait</code> without <code>Send</code> bounds means runtimes that need <code>Send</code> futures (most multi-threaded executors) impose <code>Send</code> at the bound site. tau-runtime does this; standalone consumers may not need to.</li> <li><code>is_retryable()</code> is heuristic — consumers building retry loops should expect occasional misclassification and complement with retry budgets / circuit breakers in their own layer.</li> <li>The <code>provider_specific</code> escape hatch puts pressure on the registry: every entry there is a candidate for promotion; the registry needs periodic review (no automated mechanism at v0.1).</li> <li>The <code>Sandbox</code> provisional caveat means downstream plugins targeting v0.1 sandbox accept rewrite cost at the next major. Acceptable because no real sandbox plugins exist at v0.1.</li> <li>The <code>test-fixtures</code> feature means tau-ports has two compile configurations CI must exercise (default + <code>test-fixtures</code>); the CI matrix already runs <code>--all-features</code>.</li> <li><code>ToolError::Llm</code> / <code>ToolError::Storage</code> composition means tool authors get one error type to thread, not three. The <code>From</code> impls keep <code>?</code> propagation ergonomic.</li> </ul> <h2 id="alternatives-considered-3"><a class="header" href="#alternatives-considered-3">Alternatives considered</a></h2> <ul> <li><strong><code>async-trait</code> macro</strong> for trait methods. Rejected: adds a proc-macro dependency, boxes every future (<code>Pin<Box<dyn Future>></code>), and was the workaround for the missing language feature that 1.91 now provides natively. No reason to take the dependency.</li> <li><strong>Sync trait methods, with consumers spawning their own tasks.</strong> Rejected: most LLM and storage backends are inherently async (HTTP, file I/O); forcing a sync façade pessimizes the common path.</li> <li><strong>Top-level <code>PortsError</code> umbrella.</strong> Rejected (same reasoning as ADR-0002 decision on <code>DomainError</code>): per-trait enums forever; consumers wanting "any tau-ports error" wrap their own.</li> <li><strong>Stateless <code>Tool</code> only</strong> (no <code>Session</code> associated type). Rejected: long-running stateful tools (database connection, MCP session, subprocess pool) are real; forcing them to thread state through <code>Storage</code> is awkward and pays a serialization cost per call.</li> <li><strong>Single-block <code>LlmProviderMessage</code></strong> (one content per message). Rejected: Anthropic and OpenAI both emit multi-block assistant turns (text + tool_use); a single-block shape would lose fidelity at the wire.</li> <li><strong>Streaming <code>tool_use</code> JSON to consumers as it arrives.</strong> Rejected: consumers (agents, UIs) cannot safely act on partial JSON; every consumer would have to buffer. Buffering at the plugin removes the duplication.</li> <li><strong><code>Storage</code> with TTL / transactions / watch at v0.1.</strong> Rejected: no v0.1 consumer needs them; adding now commits an API surface that may not match real backends. Additive in a later minor.</li> <li><strong>Production sandbox at v0.1.</strong> Rejected: a real sandbox is its own multi-week sub-project (Linux capabilities, seccomp, WASI research). Provisional stub keeps the trait slot reserved.</li> <li><strong>Serde-derive everything from day one.</strong> Rejected: no in-process consumer needs it; gated additive feature is the cheap path when cross-process arrives.</li> <li><strong>Mocks in a separate <code>tau-ports-test</code> crate.</strong> Rejected: extra publish target, extra SemVer surface, drift between mock and trait. The <code>test-fixtures</code> cargo feature colocates them.</li> <li><strong>No <code>provider_specific</code> escape hatch; force every param to be typed.</strong> Rejected: every new vendor would block on a tau-ports PR before plugin authors could wire up the param. The escape hatch + promotion rule keeps tau-ports out of the critical path while still curating the typed surface.</li> </ul> <div style="break-before: page; page-break-before: always;"></div><h1 id="adr-0004-tau-pkg-package-manager--public-api-storage-layout-lockfile"><a class="header" href="#adr-0004-tau-pkg-package-manager--public-api-storage-layout-lockfile">ADR-0004: tau-pkg package manager — public API, storage layout, lockfile</a></h1> <p><strong>Status:</strong> Accepted <strong>Date:</strong> 2026-04-27 <strong>Supersedes:</strong> —</p> <h2 id="context-4"><a class="header" href="#context-4">Context</a></h2> <p>tau-pkg (sub-project 3) is the package manager that implements <code>tau install</code> from git URLs, as specified by ROADMAP row 3 and Constitution G7, G8, G14. It is the first sub-project that performs real I/O: git clone, file-system mutation, TOML parse-and-write. The earlier sub-projects (tau-domain, tau-ports) were pure data structures and trait boundaries.</p> <p>Per QG18, public API additions require an ADR. This ADR records the decisions made during the sub-project 3 implementation and locks them at v0.1.</p> <p>Relevant Constitution constraints: G7 (package manager is the only way to add extensions), G8 (global + project scopes), G14 (capabilities declared at install), NG9 (no credential management), NG11 (developer tool, not an end-user consumer product), NG12 (runtime not framework).</p> <p>Note that ADR-0005 (custom serde for <code>PackageSource</code> and <code>PackageKind</code>) was filed mid-implementation as a focused fix. ADR-0004 records the broader tau-pkg decisions and cross-references ADR-0005 where relevant.</p> <h2 id="decision-4"><a class="header" href="#decision-4">Decision</a></h2> <h3 id="1-sync-public-api"><a class="header" href="#1-sync-public-api">1. Sync public API</a></h3> <p>The <code>tau_pkg</code> public surface (<code>install</code>, <code>uninstall</code>, <code>list</code>, <code>get</code>, <code>read_manifest</code>, <code>Scope::resolve</code>) is fully synchronous — no <code>async fn</code>, no <code>tokio</code> runtime dependency. The only v0.1 caller is <code>tau-cli</code>, a CLI tool where blocking on git clone is acceptable and concurrency adds no value.</p> <p>Trigger to revisit: a concrete async-server use case where multiple packages must be installed concurrently, or a long-running daemon that must remain responsive during installs.</p> <h3 id="2-shell-out-to-the-git-binary"><a class="header" href="#2-shell-out-to-the-git-binary">2. Shell out to the <code>git</code> binary</a></h3> <p>All git operations (clone, rev-parse) invoke the system <code>git</code> binary via <code>std::process::Command</code>. tau is a developer tool (NG11); users have git on PATH. This eliminates a C dependency (<code>libgit2</code> via <code>git2-rs</code>) and reduces binary size.</p> <p>Trigger to revisit: shipping pre-built binaries to non-developer environments where <code>git</code> is not guaranteed on PATH (NG11 guard).</p> <h3 id="3-storage-layout"><a class="header" href="#3-storage-layout">3. Storage layout</a></h3> <p>Packages are stored at <code><scope>/.tau/packages/<name>/<version>/</code>, where <code><scope></code> is either the project root or the global home directory. This layout supports multi-version cohabitation: two packages at different versions live under different paths and do not conflict. The <code>.tau/</code> directory is considered local state and is not committed (see decision 6).</p> <h3 id="4-scope-resolution"><a class="header" href="#4-scope-resolution">4. Scope resolution</a></h3> <p>tau-pkg walks up the directory tree looking for a <code>.tau/</code> directory (or the project root marker). If no project scope is found, it falls back to the global scope at <code>~/.tau</code>. The global directory respects <code>$TAU_HOME</code> if set, and <code>$XDG_DATA_HOME/tau</code> if <code>$TAU_HOME</code> is absent and <code>$XDG_DATA_HOME</code> is set.</p> <p>This walk-up matches developer intuitions from tools like <code>git</code> and <code>npm</code>. The two-level fallback (env vars → XDG → <code>~/.tau</code>) follows the XDG Base Directory specification.</p> <h3 id="5-lockfile-location"><a class="header" href="#5-lockfile-location">5. Lockfile location</a></h3> <p>Project lockfile: <code><project-root>/tau-lock.toml</code> — at the project root, alongside <code>tau.toml</code> and <code>Cargo.toml</code>. It is intended to be committed to version control (reproducible installs).</p> <p>Global lockfile: <code>~/.tau/tau-lock.toml</code> — inside the global tau home directory, treated as local state and not committed anywhere.</p> <p>The project lockfile lives outside <code>.tau/</code> deliberately: it must be committed while <code>.tau/</code> must not. Placing them in the same directory would require selective <code>.gitignore</code> entries that are fragile and easy to mis-configure.</p> <h3 id="6-tau-is-gitignored-local-state"><a class="header" href="#6-tau-is-gitignored-local-state">6. <code>.tau/</code> is gitignored local state</a></h3> <p>The <code>.tau/</code> directory holds installed package trees and the advisory lock file. It is local, machine-specific state and must not be committed. <code>tau init</code> (Phase 1, in <code>tau-cli</code>) will print a hint recommending that the user add <code>.tau/</code> to <code>.gitignore</code>, but does NOT modify <code>.gitignore</code> automatically. The hint is pure information; the user owns their gitignore.</p> <h3 id="7-lockfile-schema"><a class="header" href="#7-lockfile-schema">7. Lockfile schema</a></h3> <p>The lockfile is versioned (<code>schema_version = 1</code>). Each <code>[[package]]</code> entry records <code>name</code>, <code>active_version</code>, <code>source</code>, and a list of installed versions (<code>[[package.versions]]</code>). Each version entry records <code>version</code>, <code>rev</code>, <code>resolved_commit</code>, <code>sha256</code>, and <code>installed_at</code>. At v0.1 the <code>sha256</code> slot is written as an empty string. Phase 1+ <code>tau verify</code> will populate it by hashing the installed package tree. The schema version field allows future readers to detect and reject or migrate incompatible formats.</p> <h3 id="8-public-api-verbs"><a class="header" href="#8-public-api-verbs">8. Public API verbs</a></h3> <p>The v0.1 public API exports six operations: <code>install</code>, <code>uninstall</code>, <code>list</code>, <code>get</code>, <code>read_manifest</code>, and <code>Scope::resolve</code>. <code>update</code> and <code>verify</code> are deferred to Phase 1+; no stubs are included in the v0.1 crate surface to avoid committing a signature before the implementation exists.</p> <h3 id="9-manifest-location-convention"><a class="header" href="#9-manifest-location-convention">9. Manifest location convention</a></h3> <p><code>tau-pkg</code> expects the package manifest at <code>tau.toml</code> in the root of the cloned repository. This is a convention, not enforced by a config field. ADR-0002 established the manifest schema; ADR-0005 established its serde representation. tau-pkg reads the manifest using <code>read_manifest</code>, which parses to <code>UncheckedManifest</code>, validates it, and returns a <code>PackageManifest</code>.</p> <h3 id="10-no-transitive-dependency-resolution-at-v01"><a class="header" href="#10-no-transitive-dependency-resolution-at-v01">10. No transitive dependency resolution at v0.1</a></h3> <p>The <code>dependencies</code> field in <code>tau.toml</code> is parsed and stored but is informational only. <code>tau install A</code> does not automatically install A's declared dependencies. Users must <code>tau install B</code> separately. This keeps the v0.1 resolver trivially correct (no resolution graph, no version conflict analysis) and avoids committing a resolution algorithm before real use cases are known.</p> <p>Trigger to revisit: the first concrete multi-package workflow where manual chaining becomes a user pain point.</p> <h3 id="11-concurrent-install-protection"><a class="header" href="#11-concurrent-install-protection">11. Concurrent install protection</a></h3> <p>tau-pkg acquires an advisory exclusive file lock at <code>Scope::install_lock_path()</code> (project: <code><project>/.tau/locks/install.lock</code>; global: <code>~/.tau/locks/install.lock</code>) at the start of every mutating operation (<code>install</code>, <code>uninstall</code>) via <code>fs4::FileExt::lock_exclusive</code>. If <code>block_on_lock</code> is <code>false</code>, it uses <code>try_lock_exclusive</code> and returns an error immediately instead of blocking. The lock prevents two concurrent <code>tau install</code> invocations from corrupting the package tree (a real risk in CI pipelines that run multiple install scripts in parallel).</p> <p>The lock is advisory: it does not protect against callers that skip tau-pkg and write to <code>.tau/packages/</code> directly. This is acceptable; such callers are outside the defined API.</p> <h3 id="12-error-taxonomy"><a class="header" href="#12-error-taxonomy">12. Error taxonomy</a></h3> <p>Six typed error enums cover the public surface: <code>ScopeError</code>, <code>GitError</code>, <code>ManifestReadError</code>, <code>RegistryError</code> (shared by <code>list</code>, <code>get</code>, and lockfile load/save), <code>InstallError</code>, <code>UninstallError</code>. There is no top-level <code>PkgError</code> umbrella. The composing errors (<code>InstallError</code>, <code>UninstallError</code>) use <code>#[from]</code> over the leaf errors so <code>?</code> propagation works without manual mapping. This mirrors the tau-domain and tau-ports error policy (see ADR-0003 §3).</p> <h3 id="13-file-scheme-support-in-packagesource"><a class="header" href="#13-file-scheme-support-in-packagesource">13. <code>file://</code> scheme support in <code>PackageSource</code></a></h3> <p><code>PackageSource::Git</code> accepts <code>file://</code> URLs in addition to <code>https</code>, <code>http</code>, <code>ssh</code>, and <code>git</code> schemes (an extension to tau-domain made during the tau-pkg integration test phase). This enables hermetic local test fixtures (no network required in CI) and supports air-gapped installs from a local mirror.</p> <p>The <code>file://</code> scheme is a first-class extension point: <code>GitLocation::from_str</code> in tau-domain accepts <code>file</code> alongside the other schemes, and the serde round-trip (ADR-0005) preserves it as the canonical string form.</p> <h3 id="14-protocolfileallowalways-override-in-gitclone"><a class="header" href="#14-protocolfileallowalways-override-in-gitclone">14. <code>protocol.file.allow=always</code> override in <code>Git::clone</code></a></h3> <p>git 2.38+ sets <code>protocol.file.allow=user</code> by default as a mitigation for <a href="https://nvd.nist.gov/vuln/detail/CVE-2022-39253">CVE-2022-39253</a>. With that default, cloning a <code>file://</code> URL on a stock git 2.38+ installation "succeeds" with exit code 0 but writes nothing — the protocol layer silently blocks the transfer.</p> <p>tau-pkg passes <code>-c protocol.file.allow=always</code> when invoking <code>git clone</code> with a <code>file://</code> source. The CVE-2022-39253 attack vector requires <code>--recurse-submodules</code>; tau-pkg does not use submodules. The override is therefore safe for the current implementation. This analysis must be re-checked if tau-pkg ever adds submodule support.</p> <h2 id="consequences-4"><a class="header" href="#consequences-4">Consequences</a></h2> <h3 id="positive"><a class="header" href="#positive">Positive</a></h3> <ul> <li>v0.1 ships a working <code>install</code> / <code>uninstall</code> / <code>list</code> / <code>get</code> path against real git repositories, with no async runtime dependency for callers.</li> <li>73 unit tests + 24 integration tests cover the public API surface.</li> <li>Per-operation typed errors enable <code>?</code>-propagation throughout the codebase and give callers precise match arms.</li> <li><code>file://</code> support lets integration tests run hermetically in CI with no network access.</li> </ul> <h3 id="negative"><a class="header" href="#negative">Negative</a></h3> <ul> <li>The sync API blocks the calling thread during git clone. This is fine for a CLI, but embeds in an async server would require <code>spawn_blocking</code>. Trigger to revisit: a concrete async-server use case.</li> <li><code>file://</code> support requires the <code>protocol.file.allow=always</code> env override. The security analysis (CVE-2022-39253 not applicable because we do not use <code>--recurse-submodules</code>) is documented here, but must be re-checked if submodule support is ever added.</li> <li>No transitive dependency resolution: <code>tau install A</code> does not install A's declared dependencies. Users must install each dependency manually.</li> <li>v0.1 lockfile has an empty <code>sha256</code> field — Phase 1+ <code>tau verify</code> will populate it, but until then <code>tau-lock.toml</code> cannot be used to detect tampered installs.</li> </ul> <h3 id="neutral--new-obligations"><a class="header" href="#neutral--new-obligations">Neutral / new obligations</a></h3> <ul> <li>Future PRs that change tau-pkg's public API surface require their own ADRs (QG18).</li> <li>ADR-0005 governs the manifest TOML serde format for <code>PackageSource</code> and <code>PackageKind</code>; this ADR governs the broader tau-pkg API and storage decisions.</li> <li>The walk-up scope resolution algorithm is now the v0.1 standard; changing it (e.g., to never fall back to global scope) requires a new ADR.</li> <li>Adding submodule support in the future requires revisiting the <code>protocol.file.allow=always</code> security analysis in decision 14.</li> </ul> <h2 id="alternatives-considered-4"><a class="header" href="#alternatives-considered-4">Alternatives considered</a></h2> <h3 id="a-async-first-public-api"><a class="header" href="#a-async-first-public-api">A. Async-first public API</a></h3> <p>Rejected for v0.1. The only caller is <code>tau-cli</code> (a CLI, not a server), there is no concurrency value at the call site, and adding a <code>tokio</code> dependency would impose a runtime on every future crate that links tau-pkg. Cost vs. benefit is poor; see decision 1.</p> <h3 id="b-pure-rust-git-client-gitoxide-or-git2-rs"><a class="header" href="#b-pure-rust-git-client-gitoxide-or-git2-rs">B. Pure-Rust git client (gitoxide or git2-rs)</a></h3> <p>Rejected for v0.1. tau is a developer tool (NG11); developers have git on PATH. Shelling out avoids a complex C dependency (<code>libgit2</code> via <code>git2-rs</code>) and keeps binary size small. <code>gitoxide</code> is promising but not yet stable across all needed operations. Reconsider when shipping pre-built binaries to environments where git may not be present.</p> <h3 id="c-centralized-package-registry"><a class="header" href="#c-centralized-package-registry">C. Centralized package registry</a></h3> <p>Rejected permanently (NG4). Git URLs are the canonical source of truth for tau packages. A centralized registry introduces availability, trust, and governance dependencies that tau explicitly avoids.</p> <h3 id="d-top-level-pkgerror-umbrella-with-all-variants"><a class="header" href="#d-top-level-pkgerror-umbrella-with-all-variants">D. Top-level <code>PkgError</code> umbrella with all variants</a></h3> <p>Rejected. Per-operation error enums give callers more precision and allow <code>?</code>-propagation through <code>#[from]</code> without pattern-matching over a large umbrella that mixes unrelated variants. Mirrors the error policy established in tau-domain and tau-ports (ADR-0003 §3).</p> <h3 id="e-project-lockfile-under-tautau-locktoml"><a class="header" href="#e-project-lockfile-under-tautau-locktoml">E. Project lockfile under <code>.tau/tau-lock.toml</code></a></h3> <p>Rejected. Project lockfiles must be committed to enable reproducible installs; <code>.tau/</code> must not be committed (it is machine-local state). Placing the lockfile at the project root alongside <code>tau.toml</code> makes the commit vs. non-commit distinction visible and does not require complex <code>.gitignore</code> exceptions.</p> <h3 id="f-tau-init-modifies-gitignore-automatically"><a class="header" href="#f-tau-init-modifies-gitignore-automatically">F. <code>tau init</code> modifies <code>.gitignore</code> automatically</a></h3> <p>Rejected. Automatically mutating a user-owned configuration file is too magical and surprising. <code>tau-cli</code>'s <code>tau init</code> will print a hint; the user decides whether and how to update their <code>.gitignore</code>. Pure information, no mutation.</p> <h3 id="g-no-advisory-file-lock--rely-on-users-not-running-concurrent-installs"><a class="header" href="#g-no-advisory-file-lock--rely-on-users-not-running-concurrent-installs">G. No advisory file lock — rely on users not running concurrent installs</a></h3> <p>Rejected. CI pipelines routinely run multiple install scripts in parallel; a TUI tool may run an install concurrently with a background script. The <code>fs4</code> file lock costs nothing in the non-contended path and prevents silent corruption. Relying on user discipline to avoid concurrency is a footgun.</p> <h3 id="h-protocolfileallowuser-git-238-default-for-file-clones"><a class="header" href="#h-protocolfileallowuser-git-238-default-for-file-clones">H. <code>protocol.file.allow=user</code> (git 2.38+ default) for <code>file://</code> clones</a></h3> <p>Initially considered. Rejected because CI runners with stock git 2.38+ config would produce silently empty clones from <code>file://</code> test fixtures — <code>git clone</code> exits 0 but writes nothing, since the protocol layer blocks the transfer. The override to <code>always</code> is safe because tau-pkg does not use <code>--recurse-submodules</code> (the CVE-2022-39253 attack vector). See decision 14.</p> <div style="break-before: page; page-break-before: always;"></div><h1 id="adr-0005-custom-serde-for-packagesource-and-packagekind"><a class="header" href="#adr-0005-custom-serde-for-packagesource-and-packagekind">ADR-0005: Custom serde for <code>PackageSource</code> and <code>PackageKind</code></a></h1> <p><strong>Status:</strong> Accepted <strong>Date:</strong> 2026-04-27 <strong>Supersedes:</strong> —</p> <h2 id="context-5"><a class="header" href="#context-5">Context</a></h2> <p><code>PackageSource</code> and <code>PackageKind</code> are two of the most user-facing types in tau-domain: every hand-written <code>tau.toml</code> carries them. ADR-0002 ("Manifest format, capability evolution, escape-hatch policy") established the manifest format but left the per-field serde shape implicit — the v0.1 implementation derived <code>Serialize</code>/<code>Deserialize</code> for both types via <code>#[derive]</code>.</p> <p>The derived shape produces awkward TOML for two reasons:</p> <ol> <li> <p><strong><code>PackageSource::Git { location: GitLocation::Url(url::Url), rev }</code></strong> with derived serde produces nested tables because <code>url::Url</code>'s serde is itself nested. A user-written manifest needed:</p> <pre><code class="language-toml">[source] [source.Git] rev = "v0.3.0" [source.Git.location] [source.Git.location.Url] "https" = "_" # ... </code></pre> <p>This is so verbose that the existing <code>tests/manifest_roundtrip.rs</code> integration test side-stepped TOML entirely and exercised the round-trip via <code>serde_json</code>, with a comment calling the format "finicky" and the SAMPLE TOML constant marked as documentary-only.</p> </li> <li> <p><strong><code>PackageKind::Custom { kind: String }</code></strong> is a single-variant <code>#[non_exhaustive]</code> enum at v0.1. Derived serde produces <code>[kind.Custom] kind = "tool"</code> for a value that semantically reduces to the string <code>"tool"</code>.</p> </li> </ol> <p>Per QG18, "Changes to the package manifest format" require an ADR. The format users write is a function of the serde representation, so changing the representation is a manifest format change.</p> <p>The natural form a user would write is:</p> <pre><code class="language-toml">source = "https://github.com/x/y.git#main" kind = "tool" </code></pre> <p>Both types already have the necessary infrastructure for this:</p> <ul> <li><code>PackageSource: FromStr + Display</code> round-trips through <code><location>#<rev></code> (proved by <code>proptest_package_source.rs</code>).</li> <li><code>PackageKind</code> carries an inner <code>kind: String</code> that is the canonical text.</li> </ul> <h2 id="decision-5"><a class="header" href="#decision-5">Decision</a></h2> <p>We replace the derived <code>Serialize</code>/<code>Deserialize</code> on <code>PackageSource</code> and <code>PackageKind</code> with custom impls that round-trip through the string form. The change applies whenever the <code>serde</code> feature is enabled.</p> <h3 id="1-packagesource--string-form-via-displayfromstr"><a class="header" href="#1-packagesource--string-form-via-displayfromstr">1. <code>PackageSource</code> — string form via <code>Display</code>/<code>FromStr</code></a></h3> <p><code>Serialize</code> uses <code>serializer.collect_str(self)</code> (formats via <code>Display</code>, no allocation). <code>Deserialize</code> uses a <code>Visitor</code> that delegates to <code>PackageSource::from_str</code>, converting parse failures via <code>serde::de::Error::custom</code>. The visitor implements <code>visit_str</code> and <code>visit_string</code>.</p> <p>Wire form (TOML):</p> <pre><code class="language-toml">source = "https://github.com/x/y.git#main" </code></pre> <p>Wire form (JSON):</p> <pre><code class="language-json">"https://github.com/x/y.git#main" </code></pre> <p><code>GitLocation</code>'s derived serde is removed at the same time. <code>GitLocation</code> is <code>pub</code> but no longer reachable through the manifest's serde path; if a future caller needs to serialize a <code>GitLocation</code> directly, the derive can be added back at that time.</p> <h3 id="2-packagekind--inner-kind-string-form"><a class="header" href="#2-packagekind--inner-kind-string-form">2. <code>PackageKind</code> — inner-kind string form</a></h3> <p><code>Serialize</code> matches the variants exhaustively (legal inside the defining crate even on <code>#[non_exhaustive]</code> enums) and emits the inner <code>kind</code> field as a plain string. At v0.1 the only variant is <code>Custom { kind }</code>. <code>Deserialize</code> uses a <code>Visitor</code> that rejects empty strings with a useful serde error and constructs <code>Custom { kind: <input> }</code>.</p> <p>Wire form (TOML):</p> <pre><code class="language-toml">kind = "tool" </code></pre> <p>Wire form (JSON):</p> <pre><code class="language-json">"tool" </code></pre> <p>When typed <code>PackageKind</code> variants land later (e.g., <code>Tool { entrypoint: String }</code>), the custom serde will need to distinguish between a plain-string input (current path → <code>Custom { kind: <s> }</code>) and a richer table input (new typed variant). The chosen mechanism at that point will be a custom string-or-table deserializer with a tagged-discriminator inside the table form. This decision is deferred until a typed variant is concretely needed; the current single-variant case does not require pre-emptive complexity.</p> <h3 id="3-adr-0002-amendment"><a class="header" href="#3-adr-0002-amendment">3. ADR-0002 amendment</a></h3> <p>ADR-0002 referred to "the manifest format" without specifying serde representations. This ADR refines that: the manifest format is canonically what users write in <code>tau.toml</code>, and ADR-0005 documents the serde shapes for two of its fields. Future per-field shape changes follow the same pattern (an ADR per field-or-type whose representation changes).</p> <h2 id="consequences-5"><a class="header" href="#consequences-5">Consequences</a></h2> <h3 id="positive-1"><a class="header" href="#positive-1">Positive</a></h3> <ul> <li>Hand-written <code>tau.toml</code> files are dramatically simpler — the <code>source</code> and <code>kind</code> fields each fit on one line.</li> <li>The <code>tests/manifest_roundtrip.rs</code> integration test now exercises an actual TOML round-trip via the <code>SAMPLE</code> constant (rather than treating it as documentary). The "finicky TOML" caveat is gone.</li> <li>The wire form is symmetric with <code>PackageSource::FromStr</code>/<code>Display</code> — there is one canonical text representation, used consistently in CLI input, manifests, lockfiles, and error messages.</li> <li>Lockfile diffs become readable: a <code>tau-lock.toml</code> records <code>source = "https://github.com/x/y.git#main"</code> instead of nested tables.</li> </ul> <h3 id="negative-1"><a class="header" href="#negative-1">Negative</a></h3> <ul> <li>A small forward-compatibility burden when typed <code>PackageKind</code> variants land later. The mitigation is documented in §2: a string-or-table custom deserializer at that time. The burden is local to one type and one impl.</li> <li>The derived <code>Serialize</code>/<code>Deserialize</code> on <code>GitLocation</code> is removed. If a future caller needs to serialize a <code>GitLocation</code> directly (without going through <code>PackageSource</code>), the derive must be re-added. This is YAGNI for v0.1 — the only known consumer is the manifest format, and that path now goes through <code>PackageSource</code>'s custom impl.</li> </ul> <h3 id="neutral--new-obligations-1"><a class="header" href="#neutral--new-obligations-1">Neutral / new obligations</a></h3> <ul> <li>Any future ADR proposing a new variant on <code>PackageSource</code> or <code>PackageKind</code> must specify how the variant interacts with the string-form serde. The natural extension for <code>PackageKind</code> is a string-or-table form (described in §2). For <code>PackageSource</code>, future variants would extend the <code>Display</code>/<code>FromStr</code> grammar — e.g. a <code>Path</code> variant could serialize as <code>"file:///abs/path"</code> or similar — preserving the single-string property.</li> <li>Proptest coverage (added in <code>tests/proptest_package_source_serde.rs</code>) becomes a regression gate: any future change to the custom impls must keep the proptest passing.</li> <li>Lockfile schema unaffected at the field-list level: <code>LockedPackage.source: PackageSource</code> stays the same type, only the serialization differs. Existing <code>schema_version = 1</code> covers both before and after this ADR (no on-disk lockfile shipped in the wild yet).</li> </ul> <h2 id="alternatives-considered-5"><a class="header" href="#alternatives-considered-5">Alternatives considered</a></h2> <h3 id="a-keep-derived-serde-document-the-verbose-form-in-user-facing-docs"><a class="header" href="#a-keep-derived-serde-document-the-verbose-form-in-user-facing-docs">A. Keep derived serde, document the verbose form in user-facing docs</a></h3> <p>Rejected. The derived <code>[source.Git.location.Url] "https" = "_"</code> form is unworkable as a user-facing format — it leaks <code>url::Url</code>'s internal representation, requires users to know the Rust enum variant tags, and makes hand-written manifests impractical. The cost of writing custom serde impls (one per type, ~30 lines each) is far smaller than the cost imposed on every plugin author writing a manifest.</p> <h3 id="b-custom-serde-for-packagesource-only-leave-packagekind-derived"><a class="header" href="#b-custom-serde-for-packagesource-only-leave-packagekind-derived">B. Custom serde for <code>PackageSource</code> only, leave <code>PackageKind</code> derived</a></h3> <p>Rejected. <code>PackageKind</code>'s derived form (<code>[kind.Custom] kind = "tool"</code>) is also user-facing and equally awkward. The fix is symmetric — both types have a canonical inner-string form. Doing one but not the other would leave users writing a mix of natural and verbose forms in the same <code>tau.toml</code>, which is worse than either uniform alternative.</p> <h3 id="c-use-serde_withs-displayfromstr-attribute"><a class="header" href="#c-use-serde_withs-displayfromstr-attribute">C. Use <code>serde_with</code>'s <code>DisplayFromStr</code> attribute</a></h3> <p>Rejected for v0.1. Adding a <code>serde_with</code> workspace dependency for two impls is over-tooling. The explicit <code>Visitor</code> impls are ~30 lines each, easy to read, and make the validation behavior (empty-string rejection on <code>PackageKind</code>, <code>PackageSourceError</code> propagation on <code>PackageSource</code>) explicit. Reconsider if a third or fourth type needs the same treatment.</p> <h3 id="d-field-level-serdewith---on-uncheckedmanifestsource--kind"><a class="header" href="#d-field-level-serdewith---on-uncheckedmanifestsource--kind">D. Field-level <code>#[serde(with = "...")]</code> on <code>UncheckedManifest.source</code> / <code>.kind</code></a></h3> <p>Rejected. The custom (de)serialization belongs to the type, not to a particular field that uses it. Putting it on the field would force every consumer of <code>PackageSource</code> (the lockfile, future serialization paths) to repeat the attribute — and would diverge if any consumer forgot.</p> <div style="break-before: page; page-break-before: always;"></div><h1 id="adr-0006-tau-runtime-kernel--tool-capabilities-amendment"><a class="header" href="#adr-0006-tau-runtime-kernel--tool-capabilities-amendment">ADR-0006: tau-runtime kernel + Tool capabilities amendment</a></h1> <p><strong>Status:</strong> Accepted <strong>Date:</strong> 2026-04-28 <strong>Supersedes:</strong> — <strong>Amends:</strong> ADR-0003 §2 "Four trait shapes" (the <code>Tool</code> trait gains an additive <code>capabilities()</code> default method).</p> <h2 id="context-6"><a class="header" href="#context-6">Context</a></h2> <p>tau-runtime (sub-project 4, ROADMAP row 4) is the kernel that runs agents: it loads pre-constructed plugin instances, dispatches the multi-turn agent loop against an <code>LlmBackend</code> and the registered <code>Tool</code>s, enforces capability declarations at runtime, and emits structured logs. It is the first sub-project that orchestrates plugin instances rather than just defining their data shapes (tau-domain), trait boundaries (tau-ports), or installation (tau-pkg). Per ROADMAP row 4 the v0.1 scope is the <strong>solo path</strong> only; multi-agent orchestration (G10) is sub-project 5+.</p> <p>Per QG18, public API additions and plugin-trait amendments require ADRs. This ADR records BOTH the kernel design AND the additive <code>Tool::capabilities() -> &[Capability]</code> amendment to ADR-0003.
